So, I looked it up because I was curious.  Ammonium Chloride is an odorless, white powder. Solid Ammonium Chloride is used to make dry batteries and Ammonia compounds, as a soldering flux, a pickling agent in Zinc coating and tinning, and a fertilizer. Ammonium Chloride fume, a finely divided particle dispersed in air, is produced in galvanizing operations.  That Supersalt Hockey Pulverâ€¦ is basically just you eating dry batteries and fertilizer.

-saving you the google- ammonium chloride (NH4Cl) is an ionic acid (mildly acidic) that, when aqueous (aq), will conduct electricity and "quench" reacting mixtures. side effects of consuming NH4Cl are "pallor, sweating, irregular breathing, bradycardia, cardiac arrhythmias, local and general twitching, tonic convulsions and coma." (so pretty bad stuff) ALSO "ingestions can cause serious injury to the gastrointestinal tract and pulmonary systems." (and he wonders why he goes to the hospital so much) little fun side-note : the excess of ammonium from consuming NH4Cl can cause interruptions in the TCA cycles of brain cells-- therefore less ATP in the cells (which means there's less energy to go through glycolysis, CAC, ETC, etc and create energy) which will make you sleepy.  have fun with your ammonium chloride bud

29:58 HUMBUG (humbugs are a shape that tapers into a singular straight closing on 2 ends, each closing perpendicular to the other) SPOTTED!!!! likely victorian england in origin. victorian candy makers often made special die cutters for candies called drop rollers that rolled candies into specific shapes all delicately connected by a thin membrane of sugar that, when dropped, separated the drops! Most popular shapes at the height of the production and use of the drop rollers was!!!! Seashells and Humbugs! ğŸ‰ These rollers are also the reason some candies are called things like LemonDrops! Because they were originally drop roller produced candies vs cut, shapes, or broken style candies!  Modern drops are usually produced by large machines or injected into a mold, this candies likely an alternating cutter vs an industrial drop roller machine; there are still candies in england and florida(wild huh?) that use genuine antique drop rollers, cooling tables, taffy pullers, and candy rollers, they make all thier candies by hand and are usually family owned and run, they HAND MAKE candy canes! They roll out, cut, twist, and shape candy canes BY HAND! Cool huh?

A salt is just a result of an acid and base chemical reaction. Sodium Chloride is what we call table salt. The saltiness you detect on your tongue is the ionization as the sodium and chloride break down into cations and anions. There are stronger base and acid elements that will produce a stronger reaction on your tongue. ALSO, the faster a salt is dissolved by your saliva, the saltier the taste will be. Sea salt, if in an easier dissolvable form, will taste saltier than normal table salt.

Ammonium Chloride is an odorless, white powder. Solid Ammonium Chloride is used to make dry batteries and Ammonia compounds, as a soldering flux, a pickling agent in Zinc coating and tinning, and a fertilizer. Ammonium Chloride fume, a finely divided particle dispersed in air, is produced in galvanizing operations.

Hereâ€™s a Wikipedia article for ya:  Ammonium chloride is an inorganic chemical compound with the chemical formula NH4Cl, also written as [NH4]Cl. It is an ammonium salt of hydrogen chloride. It consists of ammonium cations [NH4]+ and chloride anions Clâˆ’. It is a white crystalline salt that is highly soluble in water. Solutions of ammonium chloride are mildly acidic. In its naturally occurring mineralogic form, it is known as sal ammoniac. The mineral is commonly formed on burning coal dumps from condensation of coal-derived gases. It is also found around some types of volcanic vents. It is mainly used as fertilizer and a flavouring agent in some types of liquorice. It is a product of the reaction of hydrochloric acid and ammonia.  Since Iceland is riddled with volcanoes, it wouldnâ€™t be surprised if they mine the salt and clarify it for human consumption in these candies. My guess, if it says it has ammonium chloride, itâ€™s 90% likely that itâ€™s Icelandic.

Also, as an Icelandic person, I can confirm which candies are Icelandic:  Candy 1: Icelandic. Cats do in fact live in Iceland. I own 2 of them. The reason you did not see any is because the temperatures during winter are too extreme for cats to be wandering outside for long periods of time. Candy 2: Not Icelandic (its Swedish, but a lot of Icelanders love that particular candy!). Candy 3: Icelandic. Candy 4: Not Icelandic (I have never seen bubble dust in my entire life, most likely from the UK like you said). Candy 5: Icelandic. Candy 6: Probably not Icelandic. Note: the reason why so much candy is "salty" is because of the ammonium chloride in it, also known as salmiak. That, as well as licorice, is popular in all Nordic countries! Just wanna say the extremely salty licorice, which is often known as either salmiak or salmiakki, is made with ammonium chloride, which is used to make dry batteries and ammonia compounds. So essentially the candy is licorice mixed with ammonia, thatâ€™s why it is so salty. Itâ€™s also not native to Iceland. It is originally from Finland. It started as a medicine but ended up becoming a confectionary in the 1920â€™s. (My family ancestry is largely from Nordic and Scandinavian countries and largely Finland and we very much like to stay in touch with our roots which is why I know this, I just know a bunch of random facts that has to do with Nordic and Scandinavian countries)

Ammonium Chloride is an odorless, white powder. Solid Ammonium Chloride is used to make dry batteries and Ammonia compounds, as a soldering flux, a pickling agent in Zinc coating and tinning, and a fertilizer. Ammonium Chloride fume, a finely divided particle dispersed in air, is produced in galvanizing operations. fun fact haha

licorice.
So, from a swede, if you can kinda make some sense of it then it's not Icelandic. If it sounds funny and doesn't make sense then it's probably Finnish. If you can kinda make sense of it then it's either Swedish or Norwegian but if it's also kinda funny then it's probably Danish. The trick is when the labels are in english or kinda general bc then you just gotta know where the brand comes from.   As for the raspberry + liquorice it's just a really common flavor combo, lots of liquorice both sweet, spicy, minty and everything. The salty ones are either salty liquorice or, more commonly, salmiak which is ammonium chloride. That's where the taste comes from. I personally love both liquorice and salmiak, I love it salty and a punch of flavor. Don't like sweet liquorice all that much, it's fine. Lots of people in the north here don't like either but they're also wrong.  Edit: oh goodness if mark thinks that's the saltiest candy out there he should try svenskjÃ¤vlar. Danish person jumping in for a bit of commentary, to maybe explain some of the things for Mark and the audience  4:15 Mark: *puts candy with the MOST Icelandic name in the "Not from Iceland" pile* Fun fact, there's a whole Icelandic myth about a Yule cat, a giant cat creature which comes to eat people who do not get new clothes for Christmas. And cats were most likely brought over to Iceland by the first viking settlers as cats were often used on ships for pest control, plus cats had important meaning in Norse Mythology as Freya had two big ones pulling her carriage. So cats are definitely in Iceland.  5:56 Correct. That *is* the Swedish flag. The Icelandic flag is blue with a white-bordered red cross.  17:08 The high amount of sugar in it is because it's hard candy, it's literally just sugar turned into syrup at high temperatures and then cooled down. All hard candies have that much sugar in it.  Also anything with the word salmiak or saltlakrids or any iteration of those words means it's liquorice with ammonium chloride in it, which is probably what you taste as "chemical". It's very popular here in the Nordics, we often put it inside hard candies with liquorice or tart flavours and it's definitely an acquired taste - I personally love it (surprise I'm Danish aka Nordic), can't get enough of it, but I understand if people are not fond of it. And I can't help but feel a sadistic glee when watching people try it out the first time XD  What you think of as regular liquorice is often called sweet liquorice here to distinguish it from salty licorice.  20:20 THEY ARE SUPPOSED TO BE MUSHROOMS OK?! The name means "school chalk"  45:54 Nope, Lakrids by BÃ¼low is a Danish brand making gourmet liquorice, often in combination with chocolate. Personally I'm not a fan of the combination of chocolate and liquorice, and I find Lakrids by BÃ¼low very overpriced, but if you are not the biggest fan of liquorice, there's high chance you will like that better than a lot of cheaper brands so it makes sense that you are a fan of that one.

I realize this is in the comment section of a Markiplier video but his viewers are often younger and seeing Iceland may motivate someone to go so I want to warn anyone who is naive like me about life in Iceland.   Be very careful- it isn't the utopia that tourism makes it out to be. I didn't go there as a tourist, I once had plans to live there. A lot of their equal rights talk is just lip service. Women can have a tough time and the laws aren't always fair. There's a lot of mail order brides there who become entrapped by their husbands. Lots of corruption.   Keep to yourself if you're naive, no matter what you look like. SA can happen to anyone. It's often about power and hurting a person.   It's wise NOT to drink or stay out on Saturday and Sunday nights in towns but it can even happen in daylight with people around like it did with me. I had bad situations 3 times in the first week. This isn't to say that everyone is awful- far from it! There's been good people there (like StefÃ¡n Karl) just as there has been anywhere. I met two extremely kind folks there, too. Stay safe.

15:04 there is a cocktail you can make with those candies. Itâ€™s finish its called salmiaki koshkinkorva. You shaman those candies with a hammer, dump it in a bottle of vodka, seal the bottle tightly and then toss it in your dishwasher and heave it run through a cycle. The heat from the dishwasher perfectly melts and mixes the salty licorice candies into the vodka, itâ€™s salty but also pretty smooth. It got so popular in Finland, especially amongst the youth of the country that alcoholism went on the rise, to the point it was banned for a time.

Hereâ€™s what I found out about Ammonium Chloride:  Ammonium chloride is an inorganic chemical compound with the formula NH4Cl and a white crystalline salt that is highly soluble in water. Solutions of ammonium chloride are mildly acidic. In its naturally occurring mineralogic form, it is known as sal ammoniac. The mineral is commonly formed on burning coal dumps from condensation of coal-derived gases. It is also found around some types of volcanic vents. It is mainly used as fertilizer and a flavouring agent in some types of liquorice. It is the product from the reaction of hydrochloric acid and ammonia.  ğŸ˜³

Information on   (ammonium chloride)  Ammonium chloride is an inorganic chemical compound with the formula NH4Cl and a white crystalline salt that is highly soluble in water. Solutions of ammonium chloride are mildly acidic. In its naturally occurring mineralogic form, it is known as sal ammoniac. The mineral is commonly formed on burning coal dumps from condensation of coal-derived gases. It is also found around some types of volcanic vents. It is mainly used as fertilizer and a flavouring agent in some types of liquorice. It is the product from the reaction of hydrochloric acid and ammonia.

9:05 Fun (ish) fact; They're named Jerry cans, because that design is what the German troops (Jerries) used to transport fuel in WW2. The U.S. and U.K. forces transported fuel in completely square cans, and well, those had a problem. The hard squared off edges of the Allied forces fuel cans got bent and broke at the seams all the time, so they eventually adopted the design as well.  Also, about the Ammonium Chloride. It has got the same problems as regular salt, it raises your bloodpressure. My old team-leader's husband had heart problems, and he once he was staying in a room with a young dude. And this dude had extremely high bloodpressure. Like, "OMG, why has your head not exploded!?!" high. And the doctors could not figure out why. He was in good shape, young, normal cholesterol, and exercised regularly.  Turns out, he worked at the local Haribo factory, and his favorite candy was salty licorice. And, when you work there you can eat as much as you like off the line. So, when he stopped eating the licorice, his BP went down to normal levels.

We sure do like our raspberry-liquorish here in the north. We got candy, ice-cream, and even soda. We also love salty candy, esp just eating pure ammonium chloride, like the hockey puck. That's what makes the salt-liquorish taste. Its not liquorish at all, ammonium chloride just tastes like "salty liquorish". So technically its false marketing to call ammonium chloride for liquorish. Its just used because its cheaper, and real liquorish is toxic and a few people actually die from overconsumption every year.

Ammonium chloride is an inorganic compound with the chemical formula NH4Cl. It is a white crystalline salt that is highly soluble in water and mildly acidic. It is also known as sal ammoniac and is used in various industries, including as a nitrogen supply in fertilizers and as an electrolyte in dry cells. In veterinary medicine, it is used to prevent urinary stones in sheep, goats, and cattle.

Scandinavian specialties is good
In case anyone is curious some of the reasons why Icelandic candy is so heavy on the licorice are that it is very hard to grow crops in Iceland (shocking, I know) so licorice was one of the few things the early Scandinavian settlers could grow, and at various times in history (mainly during times of economic hardship) the Icelandic government banned candy imports because they produced their own candy. So it's very much a cultural and agricultural product.

i just looked up ammonium chloride, its not meant for candy, like the sugar free gummy bears of old. it is used in batteries, soldering flux, pickling agent, fertilizer, zinc coating, medicine, glue.   It can cause gastrointestinal and pulmonary injury leading to hospitalization from dilute sources, also skin irritation. the very trace amounts are why medicine taste so horrible.  It is banned in many places because of the asthma-like reactions to it and harm to kidneys.
